	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Historical rankings of presidents of the United States</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p>
In <a href="/wiki/Political_studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Political studies">political studies</a>, surveys have been conducted in order to construct historical rankings of the success of individuals who have served as the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president of the United States</a>. Ranking systems are usually based on surveys of academic <a href="/wiki/Historian" title="Historian">historians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_scientist" class="mw-redirect" title="Political scientist">political scientists</a> or popular opinion. The rankings focus on the presidential achievements, leadership qualities, failures and faults.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gallup.com_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallup.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg/260px-2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="260" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg/390px-2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg/520px-2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2012_09_01_0037_edited-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the 1920s, sculptor <a href="/wiki/Gutzon_Borglum" title="Gutzon Borglum">Gutzon Borglum</a> and <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> selected <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> to appear on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Rushmore" title="Mount Rushmore">Mount Rushmore</a>—it later became an iconic symbol of presidential greatness.</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_findings">General findings</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> are most often listed as the three highest-rated presidents among historians. The remaining places within the Top 10 are often rounded out by <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>. More recent presidents such as <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> are often rated among the greatest in public opinion polls, but do not always rank as highly among presidential scholars and historians. The bottom 10 often include <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">Millard Fillmore</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>. Because <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> (30 days) and <a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> (200 days, incapacitated after 119 days) both died shortly after taking office, they are usually omitted from presidential rankings. Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a> died after serving as president for only 16 months, but he is usually included. In the case of these three, it is not clear if they received low rankings due to their actions as president, or because each was in office for such a limited time that it is not possible to assess them more thoroughly.
</p><p>Political scientist <a href="/wiki/Walter_Dean_Burnham" title="Walter Dean Burnham">Walter Dean Burnham</a> noted the "<a href="/wiki/Dichotomous" class="mw-redirect" title="Dichotomous">dichotomous</a> or schizoid profiles" of presidents, which can make some hard to classify. Historian <a href="/wiki/Alan_Brinkley" title="Alan Brinkley">Alan Brinkley</a> stated that "there are presidents who could be considered both failures and great or near great (for example, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Nixon</a>)". Historian and political scientist <a href="/wiki/James_MacGregor_Burns" title="James MacGregor Burns">James MacGregor Burns</a> observed of Nixon: "How can one evaluate such an <a href="/wiki/Idiosyncratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Idiosyncratic">idiosyncratic</a> president, so brilliant and so morally lacking?"<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_scholar_surveys">Notable scholar surveys</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/170px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/255px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg/340px-Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2850" data-file-height="3742" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Abraham_Lincoln_head_on_shoulders_photo_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> is often considered the greatest president for his leadership during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> and his eloquence in speeches such as the <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg_Address" title="Gettysburg Address">Gettysburg Address</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesBuchanan1860s.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/JamesBuchanan1860s.png/170px-JamesBuchanan1860s.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/JamesBuchanan1860s.png/255px-JamesBuchanan1860s.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/JamesBuchanan1860s.png/340px-JamesBuchanan1860s.png 2x" data-file-width="598" data-file-height="748" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JamesBuchanan1860s.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> is often considered the worst president for his inept leadership during the years leading up to the Civil War.</div></div></div>
<p>The 1948 poll was conducted by historian <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Sr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Sr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Sr.</a> of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The 1962 survey was also conducted by Schlesinger, who surveyed 75 historians.<sup id="cite_ref-Schlesinger,_Arthur_M_pp._12_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schlesinger,_Arthur_M_pp._12-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Schlesinger's son, <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a>, conducted another poll in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-schles96_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schles96-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>The Complete Book of U.S. Presidents</i> also gives the results of the 1982 survey, a poll of 49 historians conducted by the <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i>. A notable difference from the 1962 Schlesinger poll was the ranking of Dwight D. Eisenhower, which rose from 22nd in 1962 to 9th in 1982.
</p><p>The 1996 column shows the results from a poll conducted from 1988 to 1996 by William J. Ridings Jr. and <a href="/wiki/Stuart_B._McIver" class="mw-redirect" title="Stuart B. McIver">Stuart B. McIver</a> and published in <i>Rating The Presidents: A Ranking of U.S. Leaders, from the Great and Honorable to the Dishonest and Incompetent</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> More than 719 people took part in the poll, primarily academic historians and political scientists, although some politicians and celebrities also took part. Participants from every state were included and emphasis was placed upon getting input from female historians and "specialists in <a href="/wiki/African-American_studies" title="African-American studies">African-American studies</a>" as well as a few non-American historians. Poll respondents rated the presidents in five categories (leadership qualities, accomplishments and crisis management, political skill, appointments and character and integrity) and the results were tabulated to create the overall ranking.
</p><p>A 2000 survey by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i> consisted of an "ideologically balanced group of 132 prominent professors of history, law, and political science". This poll sought to include an equal number of <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">liberals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservatives</a> in the survey as the editors argued that previous polls were dominated by either one group or the other. According to the editors, this poll included responses from more women, minorities and young professors than the 1996 Schlesinger poll. The editors noted that the results of their poll were "remarkably similar" to the 1996 Schlesinger poll, with the main difference in the 2000 poll being the lower rankings for the 1960s presidents <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> and John F. Kennedy and higher ranking of President Ronald Reagan at 8th. Franklin D. Roosevelt still ranked in the top three.
</p><p>Another presidential poll was conducted by <i>The Wall Street Journal</i> in 2005, with <a href="/wiki/James_Lindgren" title="James Lindgren">James Lindgren</a> of <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University_Law_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern University Law School">Northwestern University Law School</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Society" title="Federalist Society">Federalist Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wall_Street_Journal_Online_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wall_Street_Journal_Online-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As in the 2000 survey, the editors sought to balance the opinions of liberals and conservatives, adjusting the results "to give Democratic- and Republican-leaning scholars equal weight". Franklin D. Roosevelt still ranked in the top three, but editor <a href="/wiki/James_Taranto" title="James Taranto">James Taranto</a> noted that Democratic-leaning scholars rated George W. Bush the sixth-worst president of all time while Republican scholars rated him the sixth-best, giving him a split-decision rating of "average".
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Siena_College_Research_Institute" title="Siena College Research Institute">Siena College Research Institute</a> of <a href="/wiki/Siena_College" title="Siena College">Siena College</a> has conducted surveys in 1982, 1990, 1994, 2002, 2010, and 2018—during the second year of the first term of each president since <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> These surveys collect presidential rankings from historians, political scientists, and presidential scholars in a range of attributes, abilities, and accomplishments.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The 1994 survey placed only two presidents, Franklin D. Roosevelt and Abraham Lincoln, above 80 points and two presidents, Andrew Johnson and Warren G. Harding, below 50 points.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2006 Siena College poll of 744 professors reported the following results:<sup id="cite_ref-Siena_2006_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siena_2006-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>"George W. Bush has just finished five years as President. If today were the last day of his presidency, how would you rank him? The responses were: Great: 2%; Near Great: 5%; Average: 11%; Below Average: 24%; Failure: 58%"</li>
<li>"In your judgment, do you think he has a realistic chance of improving his rating?" Two-thirds (67%) responded no; less than a quarter (23%) responded yes; and 10% chose "no opinion or not applicable"</li></ul>
<p>Thomas Kelly, professor emeritus of American studies at Siena College, said: "President Bush would seem to have small hope for high marks from the current generation of practicing historians and political scientists. In this case, current public opinion polls actually seem to cut the President more slack than the experts do". Douglas Lonnstrom, Siena College professor of statistics and director of the Siena Research Institute, stated: "In our 2002 presidential rating, with a group of experts comparable to this current poll, President Bush ranked 23rd of 42 presidents. That was shortly after <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">9/11</a>. Clearly, the professors do not think things have gone well for him in the past few years. These are the experts that teach college students today and will write the history of this era tomorrow".<sup id="cite_ref-Siena_2006_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siena_2006-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2010 Siena poll of 238 presidential scholars found that former president George W. Bush was ranked 39th out of 43, with poor ratings in handling of the economy, communication, ability to compromise, foreign policy accomplishments and intelligence. Meanwhile, the then-current president <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> was ranked 15th out of 43, with high ratings for imagination, communication ability and intelligence and a low rating for background (family, education and experience).<sup id="cite_ref-Siena_2010_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siena_2010-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_3&amp;CPISortType=&amp;CPIorderBy_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_3&amp;CPISortType=&amp;CPIorderBy-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 2018 Siena poll of 157 presidential scholars reported George Washington, Franklin D. Roosevelt, Abraham Lincoln, Theodore Roosevelt, and Thomas Jefferson as the top five US presidents, with <a href="/wiki/Siena_College_Research_Institute" title="Siena College Research Institute">SCRI</a> director Don Levy stating, "The top five, Mount Rushmore plus FDR, is carved in granite with presidential historians...."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Donald J. Trump—entering the SCRI survey for the first time—joined Andrew Johnson, James Buchanan, Warren G. Harding, and Franklin Pierce among the bottom five US presidents. George W. Bush, who presidential scholars had rated among the bottom five in the previous 2010 survey, improved to a position in the third quartile.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a> Survey of Presidential Leadership consists of rankings from a group of presidential historians and biographers. The C-SPAN Survey of Presidential Leadership has taken place three times: in 2000, 2009 and 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CSPAN3rdSurvey_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSPAN3rdSurvey-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The most recent survey was of 91 presidential historians, surveyed by C-SPAN's Academic Advisor Team, made up of <a href="/wiki/Douglas_G._Brinkley" class="mw-redirect" title="Douglas G. Brinkley">Douglas G. Brinkley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edna_Greene_Medford" title="Edna Greene Medford">Edna Greene Medford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Norton_Smith" title="Richard Norton Smith">Richard Norton Smith</a>. In the survey, each historian rates each president on a scale of one ("not effective") to 10 ("very effective") on presidential leadership in ten categories: Public Persuasion, Crisis Leadership, Economic Management, Moral Authority, International Relations, Administrative Skills, Relations with Congress, Vision/Setting An Agenda, Pursued Equal Justice for All and Performance Within the Context of His Times—each category is equally weighed.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The results of all three C-SPAN surveys have been fairly consistent. Abraham Lincoln has taken the highest ranking in each survey and George Washington, Franklin D. Roosevelt and Theodore Roosevelt have always ranked in the top five while James Buchanan, Andrew Johnson and Franklin Pierce have been ranked at the bottom of all three surveys.<sup id="cite_ref-CSPAN3rdSurvey_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSPAN3rdSurvey-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> daily newspaper of London asked eight of its own "top international and political commentators" to rank all 42 presidents "in order of greatness".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2011, through the agency of its United States Presidency Centre (USPC), the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_the_Study_of_the_Americas" title="Institute for the Study of the Americas">Institute for the Study of the Americas</a> (located in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_London" title="University of London">University of London</a>'s School of Advanced Study) released the first ever United Kingdom academic survey to rate presidents. This polled the opinion of British specialists in American history and politics to assess presidential performance. They also gave an interim assessment of Barack Obama, but his unfinished presidency was not included in the survey (had he been included, he would have attained eighth place overall).<sup id="cite_ref-community-languages.org.uk_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-community-languages.org.uk-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">Newsweek</a></i> magazine asked a panel of historians to rank the ten best presidents since 1900. The results showed that historians had ranked Franklin D. Roosevelt, Theodore Roosevelt, Lyndon B. Johnson, Woodrow Wilson, Harry S. Truman, John F. Kennedy, Dwight D. Eisenhower, Bill Clinton, Ronald Reagan and Barack Obama as the best since that year.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2013 <a href="/wiki/History_News_Network" title="History News Network">History News Network</a> poll of 203 American historians, when asked to rate Obama's presidency on an A–F scale, gave him a B- grade. Obama, whom historians graded using 15 separate measures plus an overall grade, was rated most highly in the categories of communication ability, integrity and crisis management; and most poorly for his relationship with Congress, transparency and accountability.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2015 poll administered by the <a href="/wiki/American_Political_Science_Association" title="American Political Science Association">American Political Science Association</a> (APSA) among political scientists specializing in the American presidency had Abraham Lincoln in the top spot, with George Washington, Franklin D. Roosevelt, Theodore Roosevelt, Thomas Jefferson, Harry S. Truman, Dwight D. Eisenhower, Bill Clinton, Andrew Jackson and Woodrow Wilson making the top 10.<sup id="cite_ref-Brandon_Rottinghaus_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brandon_Rottinghaus-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> APSA conducted a repeat of this poll in 2018, with Donald Trump appearing for the first time, in last position.<sup id="cite_ref-How_Does_Trump_Stack_Up_Against_the_Best_—_and_Worst_—_Presidents?_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-How_Does_Trump_Stack_Up_Against_the_Best_—_and_Worst_—_Presidents?-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholar_survey_results">Scholar survey results</span></h2>
<p>Within each column:<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><span style="color:blue"><b>Blue</b></span> backgrounds indicate first quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:#006600"><b>Green</b></span> backgrounds indicate second quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:darkorange"><b>Orange</b></span> backgrounds indicate third quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:#B60000"><b>Red</b></span> backgrounds indicate fourth quartile.</li></ul>
<p><span class="nomobile">Note: click the "sort" icon at the head of each column to view the rankings for each survey in numerical order.</span>

</th>
<th class="nowrap" style="line-height:99%;vertical-align:bottom;padding:21px .4em .2em;background-position:50% .4em !important;min-width:0.875em;max-width:0.875em;width:0.875em;overflow:hidden;"><div style="-webkit-writing-mode: vertical-rl; -o-writing-mode: vertical-rl; -ms-writing-mode: tb-rl;writing-mode: tb-rl; writing-mode: vertical-rl; layout-flow: vertical-ideographic;display: inline-block; -ms-transform: rotate(180deg); -webkit-transform: rotate(180deg); transform: rotate(180deg);;-ms-transform: none ;padding-left:1px;text-align:left;">APSA 2015<wbr /><sup id="cite_ref-Brandon_Rottinghaus_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brandon_Rottinghaus-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></div>
</th>

<td>42</td>
<td>40</td>
<td>42</td>

<td>43</td>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div style="padding-left: 3em; padding-right:0em; overflow: hidden;">* Ranking calculated before president had completed his term in office.
<p>Note: <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> was elected to two non-consecutive terms, serving as both the 22nd and 24th President of the United States—to date he is the only person to have achieved this distinction. Because of it, the total number of people who have served as president is one fewer than the number of presidents in order of succession.
</p><p>William Henry Harrison and James Garfield are sometimes omitted from rankings of the presidents because of the short duration of their terms in office.
</p>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Murray-Blessing_1982_survey">Murray-Blessing 1982 survey</span></h2>
<p>The Murray-Blessing 1982 survey asked historians whether they were liberal or conservative on domestic, social and economic issues.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The table below shows that the two groups had only small differences in ranking the best and worst presidents. Both groups agreed on the composition of nine of the top ten presidents (and were split over the inclusion of either Lyndon B. Johnson or Dwight D. Eisenhower) and six of the worst seven (split over <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> or <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a>).
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallup_poll">Gallup poll</span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Gallup_(company)" title="Gallup (company)">Gallup</a> poll about presidential greatness taken February 2–5, 2011, asked 1,015 adults in the United States the following question: "Who do you regard as the greatest United States president?".<sup id="cite_ref-Gallup.com_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallup.com-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>Ronald Reagan (19%)</li>
<li>Abraham Lincoln (14%)</li>
<li>Bill Clinton (13%)</li>
<li>John F. Kennedy (11%)</li>
<li>George Washington (10%)</li>
<li>Franklin Roosevelt (8%)</li>
<li>Barack Obama (5%)</li>
<li>Theodore Roosevelt (3%)</li>
<li>Harry S. Truman (3%)</li>
<li>George W. Bush (2%)</li>
<li>Thomas Jefferson (2%)</li>
<li>Jimmy Carter (1%)</li>
<li>Dwight Eisenhower (1%)</li>
<li>George H. W. Bush (1%)</li>
<li>Andrew Jackson (&lt;0.5%)</li>
<li>Lyndon B. Johnson (&lt;0.5%)</li>
<li>Richard Nixon (&lt;0.5%)</li></ol>
<p>In addition, "Other" received 1%, "None" received 1% and "No opinion" received 5%.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opinion_polls_on_recent_presidents">Public opinion polls on recent presidents</span></h2>
<p>These polls evaluate recent presidents only.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_Gallup_poll">2010 Gallup poll</span></h3>
<p>A Gallup poll taken on November 19–21, 2010, asked 1,037 Americans to say, based on what they know or remember about the nine most recent former presidents, whether they approve or disapprove of how each handled his job in office.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>John F. Kennedy (85% approval/10% disapproval)</li>
<li>Ronald Reagan (74% approval/24% disapproval)</li>
<li>Bill Clinton (69% approval/30% disapproval)</li>
<li>George H. W. Bush (64% approval/34% disapproval)</li>
<li>Gerald Ford (61% approval/26% disapproval)</li>
<li>Jimmy Carter (52% approval/42% disapproval)</li>
<li>Lyndon B. Johnson (49% approval/36% disapproval)</li>
<li>George W. Bush (47% approval/51% disapproval)</li>
<li>Richard Nixon (29% approval/65% disapproval)</li></ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_Policy_Polling">Public Policy Polling</span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Public_Policy_Polling" title="Public Policy Polling">Public Policy Polling</a> poll taken between September 8–11, 2011, asked 665 American voters, based on what they know or remember about the nine most recent former presidents, whether they hold favorable or unfavorable views of how each handled his job in office.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>John F. Kennedy (74% favorability/15% unfavorability)</li>
<li>Ronald Reagan (60% favorability/30% unfavorability)</li>
<li>Bill Clinton (62% favorability/34% unfavorability)</li>
<li>George H. W. Bush (53% favorability/35% unfavorability)</li>
<li>Gerald Ford (45% favorability/26% unfavorability)</li>
<li>Jimmy Carter (45% favorability/43% unfavorability)</li>
<li>Lyndon B. Johnson (36% favorability/39% unfavorability)</li>
<li>George W. Bush (41% favorability/51% unfavorability)</li>
<li>Richard Nixon (19% favorability/62% unfavorability)</li></ol>
<h3><span id="Vision_Critical.2FAngus_Reid_poll"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Vision_Critical/Angus_Reid_poll">Vision Critical/Angus Reid poll</span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Vision_Critical" title="Vision Critical">Vision Critical</a>/<a href="/wiki/Angus_Reid_Public_Opinion" title="Angus Reid Public Opinion">Angus Reid Public Opinion</a> poll taken on February 18–19, 2011, asked 1,010 respondents about 11 former presidents plus the current president and whether each was a good or bad president.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>John F. Kennedy (80% approval/6% disapproval)</li>
<li>Ronald Reagan (72% approval/16% disapproval)</li>
<li>Bill Clinton (65% approval/24% disapproval)</li>
<li>Dwight D. Eisenhower (61% approval/6% disapproval)</li>
<li>Harry S. Truman (57% approval/7% disapproval)</li>
<li>Jimmy Carter (47% approval/28% disapproval)</li>
<li>George H. W. Bush (44% approval/38% disapproval)</li>
<li>Barack Obama (41% approval/33% disapproval)</li>
<li>Gerald Ford (37% approval/25% disapproval)</li>
<li>Lyndon B. Johnson (33% approval/27% disapproval)</li>
<li>George W. Bush (30% approval/55% disapproval)</li>
<li>Richard Nixon (24% approval/54% disapproval)</li></ol>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2014_Quinnipiac_poll">2014 Quinnipiac poll</span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Quinnipiac_University" title="Quinnipiac University">Quinnipiac University</a> poll taken June 24–30, 2014, asked 1,446 registered voters in the United States who they thought were the best and worst presidents since <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Best President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Ronald Reagan (35%)</li><li>Bill Clinton (18%)</li><li>John F. Kennedy (15%)</li><li>Barack Obama (8%)</li><li>Dwight Eisenhower (5%)</li><li>Harry S. Truman (4%)</li><li>Lyndon B. Johnson (tie) (3%)</li><li value="7">George H. W. Bush (tie) (3%)</li><li value="9">Jimmy Carter (2%)</li><li>Richard Nixon (tie) (1%)</li><li value="10">Gerald Ford (tie) (1%)</li><li value="10">George W. Bush (tie) (1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Worst President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Barack Obama (33%)</li><li>George W. Bush (28%)</li><li>Richard Nixon (13%)</li><li>Jimmy Carter (8%)</li><li>Lyndon B. Johnson (tie) (3%)</li><li value="5">Ronald Reagan (tie) (3%)</li><li value="5">Bill Clinton (tie) (3%)</li><li value="8">Gerald Ford (tie) (2%)</li><li value="8">George H. W. Bush (tie) (2%)</li><li value="10">Dwight Eisenhower (1%)</li><li>Harry S. Truman (tie) (&lt;1%)</li><li value="11">John F. Kennedy (tie) (&lt;1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2017_Quinnipiac_poll">2017 Quinnipiac poll</span></h3>
<p>Four years later, a Quinnipiac University poll taken January 20–25, 2017, asked 1,190 voters in the United States who they thought were the best and worst presidents since World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Best President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Ronald Reagan (30%)</li><li>Barack Obama (29%)</li><li>John F. Kennedy (12%)</li><li>Bill Clinton (9%)</li><li>Dwight Eisenhower (tie) (3%)</li><li value="5">George W. Bush (tie) (3%)</li><li value="7">Harry S. Truman (tie) (2%)</li><li value="7">Lyndon B. Johnson (tie) (2%)</li><li value="7">Jimmy Carter (tie) (2%)</li><li value="7">George H. W. Bush (tie) (2%)</li><li value="11">Richard Nixon (tie) (&lt;1%)</li><li value="11">Gerald R. Ford (tie) (&lt;1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Worst President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Richard Nixon (24%)</li><li>Barack Obama (23%)</li><li>George W. Bush (22%)</li><li>Jimmy Carter (10%)</li><li>Ronald Reagan (5%)</li><li>Bill Clinton (4%)</li><li>Lyndon B. Johnson (3%)</li><li>George H. W. Bush (2%)</li><li>Gerald R. Ford (1%)</li><li>Harry S. Truman (tie) (&lt;1%)</li><li value="10">Dwight Eisenhower (tie) (&lt;1%)</li><li value="10">John F. Kennedy (tie) (&lt;1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2017_Morning_Consult_poll">2017 Morning Consult poll</span></h3>
<p>Including for the first time President Donald Trump, a <a href="/wiki/Morning_Consult" title="Morning Consult">Morning Consult</a> poll taken February 9–10, 2017, asked 1,791 registered voters in the United States, who they thought were the best and worst presidents since World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Best President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Ronald Reagan (26%)</li><li>Barack Obama (20%)</li><li>John F. Kennedy (17%)</li><li>Bill Clinton (9%)</li><li>Donald Trump (6%)</li><li>George W. Bush (tie) (2%)</li><li value="6">Harry S. Truman (tie) (2%)</li><li value="6">Jimmy Carter (tie) (2%)</li><li value="6">George H. W. Bush (tie) (2%)</li><li value="10">Richard Nixon (tie) (1%)</li><li value="10">Lyndon B. Johnson (tie) (1%)</li><li value="12">Gerald R. Ford (&lt;1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Worst President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Donald Trump (26%)</li><li>Barack Obama (25%)</li><li>Richard Nixon (13%)</li><li>George W. Bush (7%)</li><li>Bill Clinton (6%)</li><li>Jimmy Carter (5%)</li><li>George H. W. Bush (3%)</li><li>Lyndon B. Johnson (2%)</li><li>Ronald Reagan (tie) (1%)</li><li value="9">Gerald R. Ford (tie) (1%)</li><li value="9">Dwight D. Eisenhower (tie) (1%)</li><li value="9">Harry S. Truman (tie) (1%)</li><li value="13">John F. Kennedy (&lt;1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2018_Quinnipiac_poll">2018 Quinnipiac poll</span></h3>
<p>A Quinnipiac University poll taken March 3–5, 2018, asked 1,122 voters in the United States who they thought were the best and worst presidents since World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Best President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Ronald Reagan (28%)</li><li>Barack Obama (24%)</li><li>John F. Kennedy (tie) (10%)</li><li value="3">Bill Clinton (tie) (10%)</li><li value="5">Donald Trump (7%)</li><li>Dwight Eisenhower (4%)</li><li>Harry S. Truman (tie) (3%)</li><li value="7">Jimmy Carter (tie) (3%)</li><li value="9">Lyndon B. Johnson (2%)</li><li>George H. W. Bush (tie) (1%)</li><li value="10">Richard Nixon (tie) (1%)</li><li value="10">George W. Bush (tie) (1%)</li><li value="13">Gerald R. Ford (&lt;1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<div style="margin:0 1em;; display: inline-block;">
<p><b>Worst President since World War II:</b>
</p>
<div><ol><li>Donald Trump (41%)</li><li>Barack Obama (21%)</li><li>Richard Nixon (10%)</li><li>Jimmy Carter (8%)</li><li>George W. Bush (6%)</li><li>Bill Clinton (4%)</li><li>Lyndon B. Johnson (tie) (2%)</li><li value="7">Ronald Reagan (tie) (2%)</li><li value="9">Gerald R. Ford (1%)</li><li>Harry S. Truman (tie) (&lt;1%)</li><li value="10">Dwight Eisenhower (tie) (&lt;1%)</li><li value="10">John F. Kennedy (tie) (&lt;1%)</li><li value="10">George H. W. Bush (tie) (&lt;1%)</li></ol></div>
</div>
<h2><span id="Siena_College_Research_Institute.2C_Presidential_Expert_Poll_of_2010"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Siena_College_Research_Institute,_Presidential_Expert_Poll_of_2010">Siena College Research Institute, Presidential Expert Poll of 2010</span></h2>
<p><b>Abbreviations</b>
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">
<ul><li>Bg = Background</li>
<li>PL = Party leadership</li>
<li>CAb = Communication ability</li>
<li>RC = Relations with Congress</li>
<li>CAp = Court appointments</li>
<li>HE = Handling of economy</li>
<li>L = Luck</li>
<li>AC = Ability to compromise</li>
<li>WR = Willing to take risks</li>
<li>EAp = Executive appointments</li>
<li>OA = Overall ability</li>
<li>Im = Imagination</li>
<li>DA = Domestic accomplishments</li>
<li>Int = Integrity</li>
<li>EAb = Executive ability</li>
<li>FPA = Foreign policy accomplishments</li>
<li>LA = Leadership ability</li>
<li>IQ = Intelligence</li>
<li>AM = Avoid crucial mistakes</li>
<li>EV = Experts' view</li>
<li>O = Overall</li></ul>
</div>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><span style="color:blue"><b>Blue</b></span> backgrounds indicate first quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:#006600"><b>Green</b></span> backgrounds indicate second quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:darkorange"><b>Orange</b></span> backgrounds indicate third quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:#B60000"><b>Red</b></span> backgrounds indicate fourth quartile.</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2017_C-SPAN_Presidential_Historian_Survey">2017 C-SPAN Presidential Historian Survey</span></h2>
<p><b>Abbreviations</b>
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">
<ul><li>PP = Public persuasion</li>
<li>CL = Crisis leadership</li>
<li>EM = Economic management</li>
<li>MA = Moral authority</li>
<li>IR = International relations</li>
<li>AS = Administrative skills</li>
<li>RC = Relations with Congress</li>
<li>VSA = Vision/Setting an agenda</li>
<li>PEJ = Pursued equal justice for all</li>
<li>PCT = Performance within context of times</li>
<li>O = Overall</li></ul>
</div>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<ul><li><span style="color:blue"><b>Blue</b></span> backgrounds indicate first quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:#006600"><b>Green</b></span> backgrounds indicate second quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:darkorange"><b>Orange</b></span> backgrounds indicate third quartile.</li>
<li><span style="color:#B60000"><b>Red</b></span> backgrounds indicate fourth quartile.</li></ul>
<p>Source:<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>

<p><span id="Memorability_of_the_presidents"></span>
</p>
<h2><span id="Siena_College_Research_Institute.2C_Presidential_Expert_Poll_of_2018"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Siena_College_Research_Institute,_Presidential_Expert_Poll_of_2018">Siena College Research Institute, Presidential Expert Poll of 2018</span></h2>
<p>On February 13, 2019, Siena released its 6th presidential poll.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The poll was initiated in 1982 and occurs one year into the term of each new president. It is currently a survey of 157 presidential scholars, across a range of leadership parameters.
</p><p>The ranking gave the top five spots to George Washington, Franklin Roosevelt (FDR), Abraham Lincoln, Theodore (Teddy) Roosevelt and Thomas Jefferson. This top five, described as Mt. Rushmore plus FDR,  was consistent with prior surveys. Washington had been ranked fourth in all previous surveys, and FDR first.
</p><p><b>Abbreviations</b>
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 22em; -webkit-column-width: 22em; column-width: 22em;">
<ul><li>Bg = Background</li>
<li>Im = Imagination</li>
<li>Int = Integrity</li>
<li>IQ = Intelligence</li>
<li>L = Luck</li>
<li>WR = Willing to take risks</li>
<li>AC = Ability to compromise</li>
<li>EAb = Executive ability</li>
<li>LA = Leadership ability</li>
<li>CAb = Communication ability</li>
<li>OA = Overall ability</li>
<li>PL = Party leadership</li>
<li>RC = Relations with Congress</li>
<li>CAp = Court appointments</li>
<li>HE = Handling of economy</li>
<li>EAp = Executive appointments</li>
<li>DA = Domestic accomplishments</li>
<li>FPA = Foreign policy accomplishments</li>
<li>AM = Avoid crucial mistakes</li>
<li>EV = Experts' view</li>
<li>O = Overall</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorability_of_the_presidents">Memorability of the presidents</span></h2>
<p>In November 2014, <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Roediger_III" title="Henry L. Roediger III">Henry L. Roediger III</a> and K. Andrew DeSoto published a study in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> asking research subjects to name as many presidents as possible.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> They reported data from three generations as well as from an online survey conducted in 2014. The percentage of participants in the online survey sample who could name each president was the following:
</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 15em; -webkit-column-width: 15em; column-width: 15em;">
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> (100%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> (96%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> or <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> (95%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> (94%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> (88%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> (83%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> (82%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> (79%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> (72%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> (66%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> (62%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> or <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> (60%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> or <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a> (56%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> (54%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a> (50%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> (47%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> (42%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andrew_Johnson" title="Andrew Johnson">Andrew Johnson</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> (41%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> (39%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> (38%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a> (38%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> (30%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> (29%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> (22%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_A._Garfield" title="James A. Garfield">James A. Garfield</a> (19%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a> (17%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Warren_G._Harding" title="Warren G. Harding">Warren G. Harding</a> (16%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_McKinley" title="William McKinley">William McKinley</a> (15%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Tyler" title="John Tyler">John Tyler</a> (12%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> (12%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> (11%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Harrison" title="William Henry Harrison">William Henry Harrison</a> or <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a> (11%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Martin_Van_Buren" title="Martin Van Buren">Martin Van Buren</a> (11%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rutherford_B._Hayes" title="Rutherford B. Hayes">Rutherford B. Hayes</a> (10%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zachary_Taylor" title="Zachary Taylor">Zachary Taylor</a> (10%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Millard_Fillmore" title="Millard Fillmore">Millard Fillmore</a> (8%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Franklin_Pierce" title="Franklin Pierce">Franklin Pierce</a> (7%)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chester_A._Arthur" title="Chester A. Arthur">Chester A. Arthur</a> (7%)</li></ol>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Excluded_groups_ranking_approach">Excluded groups ranking approach</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Herbert_Donald" title="David Herbert Donald">David H. Donald</a>, noted biographer of Abraham Lincoln, relates that when he met John F. Kennedy in 1961, Kennedy voiced his deep dissatisfaction and resentment with historians who had rated some of his predecessors. Kennedy said: "No one has a right to grade a president—even poor James Buchanan—who has not sat in his chair, examined the mail and information that came across his desk, and learned why he made his decisions".<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Historian and political scientist <a href="/wiki/Julian_E._Zelizer" title="Julian E. Zelizer">Julian E. Zelizer</a> argues that traditional presidential rankings explain little concerning actual presidential history and that they are "weak mechanisms for evaluating what has taken place in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Zelizer_02_21_2011_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zelizer_02_21_2011-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ivan_Eland.27s_Recarving_Rushmore_.282008.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ivan_Eland's_Recarving_Rushmore_(2008)">Ivan Eland's <i>Recarving Rushmore</i> (2008)</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_in_the_United_States" title="Libertarianism in the United States">Libertarian</a> political commentator <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Eland" title="Ivan Eland">Ivan Eland</a> wrote a book titled <i>Recarving Rushmore</i> (2008; updated 2014) in which he wrote that historians' criteria are poor in their capacity to reflect presidents' actual services to the country. In the book, Eland chose to rate 40 presidents on the basis of whether their policies promoted prosperity, liberty and <a href="/wiki/United_States_non-interventionism" title="United States non-interventionism">non-interventionism</a> as well as modest executive roles for themselves—his final rankings varied significantly from those of most scholars.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Racial_Equality_Assessments">Racial Equality Assessments</span></h3>
<p>In 2002, <a href="/wiki/Ron_Walters" title="Ron Walters">Ron Walters</a>, former director of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Maryland" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Maryland">University of Maryland</a>'s African American Leadership Institute, stated that presidents ranked by how each one balanced the interests of majority interests and the interests of excluded groups was practical in respect to American debate on racial politics. presidents have traditionally been ranked on personal qualities and their leadership ability to solve problems that move the nation in a positive direction. Walters stated there was a qualitative difference between white and African-American intellectuals in evaluating presidents. In the 1996 <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">New York Times</a></i> poll by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_M._Schlesinger_Jr." title="Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.">Arthur M. Schlesinger Jr.</a>, 31 white historians and one black historian ranked presidents on differing categories of greatness. In a survey done by professors Hanes Walton Jr. and Robert Smith and in their text book <i>American Politics And The African American Quest For Universal Freedom</i>, 44 African-American political scientists and historians ranked presidents in terms of racial attitudes and racial legislation proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Individual presidents' attitudes, policies and perspectives were historically ranked in five categories: White Supremacist; Racist; Racially Neutral; Racially Ambivalent; Antiracist.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Northwestern_Presidential_Leadership_on_Diversity_and_Inclusion_Survey_.282019.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Northwestern_Presidential_Leadership_on_Diversity_and_Inclusion_Survey_(2019)">Northwestern Presidential Leadership on Diversity and Inclusion Survey (2019)</span></h4>
<p>In May 2019, the <a href="/wiki/Weinberg_College_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="Weinberg College of Arts and Sciences">Weinberg College of Arts and Sciences</a>, "in conjunction the Dr. <a href="/wiki/Tina_Green" title="Tina Green">Christina Greer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Fordham_University" title="Fordham University">Fordham University</a>, the Center for the Study of Diversity and Democracy’s conducted a poll of 113 academic researchers and asked them to rate the 14 modern presidents on both their overall leadership and rhetoric on diversity and inclusion using a scale ranging from 0 to 100." <sup id="cite_ref-:0_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Survey respondents were significantly more <a href="/wiki/Liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Liberalism in the United States">liberal</a> than the national average, with "with only 13 percent of the respondents describing themselves as either moderate, slightly conservative, or conservative." However, "similar patterns of ratings [were stated to be found] across the ideological spectrum".<sup id="cite_ref-:0_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span id="Overall_.28Performance_.2B_Diversity_and_Inclusion_Score.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Overall_(Performance_+_Diversity_and_Inclusion_Score)">Overall (Performance + Diversity and Inclusion Score)</span></h5>
<p>1.) <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin Roosevelt</a> (83/100)
</p><p>2.) <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> (77/100)
</p><p>3.) <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> (69/100)
</p><p>4.) <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> (62/100)
</p><p>5.) <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John Kennedy</a> (61/100)
</p><p>6.) <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry Truman</a> (57/100)
</p><p>7.) <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> (54.4/100)
</p><p>8.) <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> (54.1/100)
</p><p>9.) <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> (50/100)
</p><p>10.) <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> (49/100)
</p><p>11.) <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> (39/100)
</p><p>12.) <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> (38/100)
</p><p>13.) <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> (32/100)
</p><p>14.) <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> (11/100)
</p>
<h5><span id="Diversity_and_Inclusion_Leadership_Score_.28Without_Including_Overall_Leadership.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversity_and_Inclusion_Leadership_Score_(Without_Including_Overall_Leadership)">Diversity and Inclusion Leadership Score (Without Including Overall Leadership)</span></h5>

</p><p>5.) <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> (40/100)
</p><p>6.) <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> (34/100)

<li id="cite_note-TylerParty-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-TylerParty_31-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-TylerParty_31-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-TylerParty_31-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-TylerParty_31-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Tyler was elected on the Whig ticket as Harrison's vice president, but Tyler became an <a href="/wiki/Independent_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Independent (politics)">independent</a> after the Whigs expelled him from the party in 1841.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-JohnsonParty-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-JohnsonParty_32-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JohnsonParty_32-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JohnsonParty_32-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-JohnsonParty_32-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Johnson was a former War Democrat elected on the National Union ticket as Lincoln's vice president, but by 1868 the National Union Party disbanded.</span>
</li>
